<span id="freeTextContainer8767307730291916377">"Last night I dreamt I went to Manderley again."<br /><br />So the second Mrs. Maxim de Winter remembered the chilling events that led her down the turning drive past the beeches, white and naked, to the isolated gray stone manse on the windswept Cornish coast. With a husband she barely knew, the young bride arrived at this immense estate, only to be inexorably drawn into the life of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8767307730291916377">"Last night I dreamt I went to Manderley again."<br /><br />So the second Mrs. Maxim de Winter remembered the chilling events that led her down the turning drive past the beeches, white and naked, to the isolated gray stone manse on the windswept Cornish coast. With a husband she barely knew, the young bride arrived at this immense estate, only to be inexorably drawn into the life of the first Mrs. de Winter, the beautiful Rebecca, dead but never forgotten...her suite of rooms never touched, her clothes ready to be worn, her servant -- the sinister Mrs. Danvers -- still loyal. And as an eerie presentiment of the evil tightened around her heart, the second Mrs. de Winter began her search for the real fate of Rebecca...for the secrets of Manderley.</span>

<div class="allCommunityAnswers">
<div class="communityAnswer">
<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a href="/user/show/28695735-beth" class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" alt="" /></a>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/28695735-beth">Beth</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I personally love her because I can relate to her so much, like her I hate to meet new people and I can be socially awkward, and I sometimes feel like<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I personally love her because I can relate to her so much, like her I hate to meet new people and I can be socially awkward, and I sometimes feel like I have a lack of identity and am overshadowed by friends and family (though no ex-wife of my husband!).<br /><br />It's quite simply really, but two times I think she should have acted otherwise are when she hid the broken ornament instead of owning up straight away and climbed out of the window so not to have to meet Beatrice and Giles. Both those times really annoyed me!<br />And because I felt really sorry for her when it happened, I wish she'd asked Maxim or someone about the dress before wearing it for the ball!<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/2081038?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 18, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/94602-kelly" class="user" title="Kelly">Kelly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12873.Rebecca" title="Paperback 9781844080380" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of gothic lit.</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer2081038">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3797725761498670399">This is it. THE delicious, curl up next to the fire under a blanket with tea book. THE windowsill on a rainy day with your pet book. THE stay up all night book. A chill goes down your spine (but in a good way!) while reading it. It is a masterpiece of gothic literature, the inheritor of the tradition of novels like Wuthering Heights and Jane Eyre. I'd call it the 20th Century Jane Eyre, actually, with a modernist twist. It is written so that the characters and events come to seem quite believabl</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3797725761498670399">This is it. THE delicious, curl up next to the fire under a blanket with tea book. THE windowsill on a rainy day with your pet book. THE stay up all night book. A chill goes down your spine (but in a good way!) while reading it. It is a masterpiece of gothic literature, the inheritor of the tradition of novels like Wuthering Heights and Jane Eyre. I'd call it the 20th Century Jane Eyre, actually, with a modernist twist. It is written so that the characters and events come to seem quite believable in the context even while they slowly make the hairs on the back of your necks stand on end. Whether you're generally a fan of mystery, romance or thrillers, this book is quite simply a delicious read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3797725761498670399">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_508731135">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/508731135" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten" class="left imgcol" title="Jeffrey Keeten"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" alt="Jeffrey Keeten" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/508731135?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 14, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten" class="user" title="Jeffrey Keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12873.Rebecca" title="Paperback 9781844080380" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer508731135">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17742780125762314615"><strong>”Last night I dreamt I went to Manderley again".</strong><br /><br />This is one of the more famous lines in literature certainly it belongs in the same conversation as <em>Call me Ishmael</em>. Even to people who have never read the book or seen the excellent movie by Alfred Hitchcock might have a glimmer of recognition at the mention of a place called Manderley. Daphne du Maurier leased a place called Menabilly which became the basis for the fictional Manderley. Aren’t we glad she changed the name? Just say Manderley a few</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17742780125762314615"><strong>”Last night I dreamt I went to Manderley again".</strong><br /><br />This is one of the more famous lines in literature certainly it belongs in the same conversation as <em>Call me Ishmael</em>. Even to people who have never read the book or seen the excellent movie by Alfred Hitchcock might have a glimmer of recognition at the mention of a place called Manderley. Daphne du Maurier leased a place called Menabilly which became the basis for the fictional Manderley. Aren’t we glad she changed the name? Just say Manderley a few times and then say Menabilly a few times. If you are like me you linger over the vowels and consonants of Manderley and with Menabilly you just want it off your tongue as quickly as possible.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.beta.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/duMaurier_zpsdc7606e3.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="duMaurier_zpsdc7606e3 photo duMaurier_zpsdc7606e3.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380405499r/789983._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Daphne du Maurier on the staircase at Menabilly</strong><br /><br />The narrator, a young woman of 21, is never formally introduced to us. She is a companion for an odious American woman named Mrs. Van Hoppers. They are in Monte Carlo and when Mrs. Van Hoppers comes down with an illness inspired more by boredom than by a virus or bacteria our narrator finds herself free to spend time with the widower Maximilian de Winter. He is famous, but his house, Manderley is even more famous. Parties on a Gatsby scale, beautiful landscaping, and of course the architecture of a grand English estate have made Manderley a most coveted invitation. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.beta.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/MaximiliandeWinter_zps95cde1de.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="MaximiliandeWinter_zps95cde1de photo MaximiliandeWinter_zps95cde1de.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380405499r/789984._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Laurence Olivier as Maximilian de Winter</strong><br /><br />After a whirlwind romance, the dashing de Winter sweeps the impressionable young lady off her feet, pries her loose from the services of Mrs. Van Hoppers, and marries her. He is distant, moody, and yet charming more like a father, he is 42, than a husband, but our young heroine is enamored with the idea of being the mistress of Manderley. Now she has a name, Mrs. de Winter, and maybe to add a bit of obscurity to an already anemic personality du Maurier never shares her given name with us. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.beta.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Menabilly_zpsb6e5872d.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Menabilly_zpsb6e5872d photo Menabilly_zpsb6e5872d.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380405499r/789985._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Daphne du Maurier and children at Menabilly the inspiration for Manderley</strong><br /><br />Daphne du Maurier comes from a famous family. Her grandfather was the famous writer and Punch cartoonist George du Maurier. Her father was a prominent stage manager named Sir Gerald du Maurier and her mother was the actress Muriel Beaumont. Daphne had <em>”breeding, brains, and beauty”</em>which is used in reference to the character Rebecca as well, and luckily du Maurier chose to do more with this trilogy of assets than the character. Du Maurier married Lieutenant General Sir Frederick ‘Boy’ Browning whose exploits during Operation Market Garden were made into a film <em>A Bridge too Far</em>.<br /><br />The newly minted Mrs. de Winter arrives at Manderley with nervous excitement. She is well aware of her shortcomings. She is too shy, too young, too trusting, and though she is pretty she can not compete with the legendary Rebecca de Winter and her haunting beauty.<br /><br /><em>”Rebecca, always Rebecca. Wherever I walked in Manderley, wherever I sat, even in my thought and in my dreams, I met Rebecca. I knew her figure now, the long slim legs, the small and narrow feet. Her shoulders broader than mine, the capable clever hands. Hands that could steer a boat, could hold a horse. Hands that arranged flowers, made the models of ships, and wrote ‘Max from Rebecca’ on the fly-leaf of a book. I knew her face too, small and oval, the clear white skin, the cloud of dark hair. I knew the scent she wore, I could guess her laughter and her smile. If I heard it, even among a thousand others, I should recognise her voice. Rebecca, always Rebecca. I should never be rid of Rebecca.”</em><br /><br />Waiting for Mrs. de Winter is the number one fan and torchbearer of Rebecca, Mrs. Danvers. Despite the best efforts of our young lady, she is fighting a losing battle trying to win over Mrs. Danvers by being deferential. Mrs. Danvers is loyal to the ghostly presence of Rebecca even to the point of preserving her room and possessions as they were when she was alive. The more that the new Mrs. de Winter concedes the less respect she feels she has to show to the new mistress of the house. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.beta.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/MrsDanvers_zpsd186abf8.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="MrsDanvers_zpsd186abf8 photo MrsDanvers_zpsd186abf8.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380405499r/789986._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Mrs. Danvers played by Judith Anderson and Mrs. de Winter played by Joan Fontaine in the 1940 Alfred Hitchcock movie</strong><br /><br /><em>”We stood there by the door, staring at one another. I could not take my eyes away from hers. How dark and sombre they were in that white skull’s face of hers, how malevolent, how full of hatred.”</em><br /><br />You will feel yourself wanting to cheer as our heroine begins to gain confidence and as she begins to grow into her role we see Mrs. Danvers start to diminish and with her the haunting presence of Rebecca. Of course just as things start to go right, things start to go very wrong. <br /><br />I was really surprised to learn that an edition of <em>Rebecca</em> was used as the key to a code book by the Germans during World War Two. It is not believed that the book was ever used for passing information because a captured radio section made the Germans suspect that the book, as a code, had been compromised. Ken Follett used this idea in his book <em>The Key to Rebecca</em>. Other influences of possibly du Maurier’s most famous character creation, show up in Stephen King’s <em>Bag of Bones</em> when Mrs. Danvers is portrayed as the boogeyman. Jasper Ffordes clones an army of Mrs. Danvers in his Thursday Next series that sends a chill down the backs of the characters of those books. <br /><br />There is much made of flowers and landscaping in this book. The English do love their rose gardens and when my backyard is in full bloom it is without reservation that I can share how much pleasure looking at and moving among that bounty of blooms gives me. <br /><br /><em>”No wild flowers came in the house at Manderley. He had special cultivated flowers, grown for the house alone, in the walled garden. A rose was one of the few flowers, he said, that looked better picked than growing. A bowl of roses in a drawing-room had a depth of colour and scent they had not possessed in the open. There was something rather blowsy about roses in full bloom, something shallow and raucous, like women with untidy hair. In the house they became mysterious and subtle.”</em><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.beta.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/DaphneduMaurier_zps50770ae5.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo DaphneduMaurier_zps50770ae5.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380405499r/789987._SX540_SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Daphne du Maurier</strong><br /><br />You will feel the building tension as du Maurier drops hints of something sinister surrounding the walls of Manderley. For me, the sign of a well written book is the fact that I was on the edge of my seat despite having watched the movie several times. I was ensnared by the plot, feeling the same anxiety for the characters that I would have if they had been living breathing creatures in my own sphere of the universe. The character studies explored in this book have turned out to be an important addition to the hall of fame of literary characters. You will not forget Mrs. Danver’s spiteful, insidious behavior or the tortured, Heathcliffesque Maximilian de Winter or the numerous supporting cast that adds color and substance to the shadows of the plot. If you like gothic romance with your cup of Earl Grey you will find this book an indispensable part of your library, kept ready to hand for those days when you want to be swept away from a dreary sky and a rain splattered window. <br /><br /><em>”The road to Manderley lay ahead. There was no moon. The sky above our heads was inky black. But the sky on the horizon was not dark at all. It was shot with crimson, like a splash of blood. And the ashes blew towards us with the salt wind from the sea.”</em><br /><br />Check out my book and movie reviews at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com" target="_blank">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17742780125762314615">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_894335740">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/894335740" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/18104163-navessa" class="left imgcol" title="Navessa"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1444162983p2/18104163.jpg" alt="Navessa" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/894335740?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 30, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/18104163-navessa" class="user" title="Navessa">Navessa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/17899948-rebecca" title="ebook 9780316323703" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/18104163-navessa?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18104163-navessa?shelf=divisive-characters" class="actionLinkLite">divisive-characters</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18104163-navessa?shelf=books-everyone-loves-but-i-hate" class="actionLinkLite">books-everyone-loves-but-i-hate</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18104163-navessa?shelf=not-a-friend-of-feminism" class="actionLinkLite">not-a-friend-of-feminism</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18104163-navessa?shelf=gd-mary-sues" class="actionLinkLite">gd-mary-sues</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18104163-navessa?shelf=insufferable-mc" class="actionLinkLite">insufferable-mc</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18104163-navessa?shelf=trolls-love-me" class="actionLinkLite">trolls-love-me</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18104163-navessa?shelf=glorifies-abusive-relationships" class="actionLinkLite">glorifies-abusive-relationships</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer894335740">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12951354594060188729"><strong>Well…this is awkward.</strong> <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1383760554ra/6658283.gif" /><br /><br />So, most of my friends love this book. Naturally, I wanted to as well. I blame the herd mentality. <br /><br />Baaah. <br /><br />Did I love this book? At times, yes. Did I also loathe this book? At times, yes. It’s made deciding on a rating a much more daunting task than I normally face. After reflecting on it for some time, and re-reading my f-bomb laden notes, I’m going with two stars, because as a whole, I did not enjoy this. <br /><br />While I greatly detested some aspects, I can still recognize gorgeou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12951354594060188729"><strong>Well…this is awkward.</strong> <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1383760554ra/6658283.gif" /><br /><br />So, most of my friends love this book. Naturally, I wanted to as well. I blame the herd mentality. <br /><br />Baaah. <br /><br />Did I love this book? At times, yes. Did I also loathe this book? At times, yes. It’s made deciding on a rating a much more daunting task than I normally face. After reflecting on it for some time, and re-reading my f-bomb laden notes, I’m going with two stars, because as a whole, I did not enjoy this. <br /><br />While I greatly detested some aspects, I can still recognize gorgeous prose when I see it. Honestly, I almost gave this three stars for the writing alone, because it’s so beautiful that it becomes distracting, and when you pair this with a hauntingly gothic setting? Magic.<br /><br /><em>“Nature had come into her own again and, little by little, in her stealthy, insidious way had encroached upon the drive with long, tenacious fingers.”</em><br /><br />What ruined this for me were the attitudes and the actions of those depicted within it. I’m not one of those readers that have to love characters to enjoy a story or appreciate its message. There have been quite a few instances where I’ve rated a book highly even when I hated every single person in it. That’s because while I didn’t necessarily agree with what they were doing, or their thought processes, I understood them on some level. <br /><br />Sadly, this is not the case with <em>Rebecca</em>. The female lead, who is also the narrator, is left without a name throughout. Upon reading the afterward, I’m wondering if that’s because it would have been all too obvious if the author had named her Daphne. She is the very definition of a Mary Sue, taking almost entirely after the person who created her. But, for the sake of this review, let’s call the MC Not Cory. I think it has a catchy ring to it. It’s also fitting, as she is the complete and total opposite of me in almost every way imaginable. <br /><br />When the book opens, Not Cory is reflecting on her life, and the remaining pages focus on the events she’s reliving. It all begins in Monte Carlo, with her as the companion to a tactless and garish woman of means. A chance encounter has her bumping into a wealthy Englishman named Maxim de Winter, a man twenty years her senior and recently widowed. <br /><br />What follows is a whirlwind courtship set against the backdrop of the south of France. Sounds lovely, doesn’t it? Er, not so much. This part of the book served as nothing more than a reminder of how foolish first love can make us. My hat is off to Du Maurier for her flawless portrayal of this time in our lives, when your future is spread out in front of you and all your fantasies still seem tangible. <br /><br />What made it that much more difficult to read was the MC. Not Cory is a dreamer; she lives largely inside of her head, and the smallest thing, from her companion choosing to host a cocktail party, to the rising of the sun, can send her mind spinning as she plays out the infinite consequences of these things. <br /><br />This tendency becomes obsessive when Maxim enters the picture. Where she is both immature and naïve, he is complicated and divided. Her happiness becomes dependent on his smiles, her misery decided by a harsh word. Thanks to their age difference, he’s forgotten what this is like, how raw and all-consuming first love can be, and he’s careless with her feelings because of it. He takes complete advantage of her throughout, and I hated him almost from the point of his introduction. <br /><br />The singular reason that he’s even attracted to her at all is because <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[she is the antithesis of his late wife. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> So I’m sorry to all the Maxim shippers, but I can find nothing redeeming or romantic about an older man who so casually mistreats his innocent young wife. <br /><br />Not Cory might be naïve, but she’s not an utter fool. Her introspectiveness makes her a keen observer, and even in their early days together, she realizes that something isn’t right between her and Maxim. She senses it should be different, has premonitions of what’s to come. After their honeymoon, they head home, to Manderley where all becomes clear. <br /><br />Rebecca. Rebecca. <em>Rebecca. <strong>Rebecca. <u>REBECCA!</u></strong></em><br /><br />The dead wife. She’s there with them, always. Both the servants of the household, and the surrounding villagers preferred her and her outgoing nature, her wild parties, the way she could draw one out of themselves and make them feel as though they were a trusted friend, to Not Cory and her shy, withdrawn nature. It doesn’t help matters that she was tall, gorgeous, refined, and well bred, and that almost everyone Not Cory meets feels the need to remind her of this. <br /><br />She feels Rebecca hanging over her marriage like a ghoul, dogging her steps throughout her new home, distracting and beguiling her husband from beyond the grave. So it’s understandable that after a few short weeks at Manderley, Not Cory begins to further withdraw from her surroundings, turn more introspective, begin to question everything about her marriage and the man she’s attached herself to. <br /><br />At this point, the book turns into a slow motion train wreck. You see how easily she’s manipulated by the aging Mrs. Danvers, Rebecca’s old maid, by her husband, by the others around her, and you just want to shake her out of it. Instead of character progression, you get regression, and watching it all unfold is frustrating to say the least. I kept waiting for her to grow a spine, for her to start questioning things, to stand up for herself. Sadly, this never happens. <br /><br />And all the while, there’s Maxim, patting her head like a dog if she pleases him, frowning and becoming withdrawn if she doesn’t. I saw too many signs of a perfect victim within his wife, and it greatly disturbed me. She became constantly aware of his moods, adjusted her own behaviors to compensate for them, steered conversations if she worried that they might be heading down roads that might upset him. <br /><br />Maxim, Maxim, <em>Maxim, <strong>Maxim, <u>MAXIM!</u></strong></em> <br /><br />Less than half of the way through, I had every single plot twist unraveled. It made for a rather anticlimactic finish, and I found myself skimming through large sections of Not Cory’s infuriatingly weak inner monologue. <br /><br />The biggest “revelation” of them all almost caused me to rage quit, but I had come too far. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ Maxim reveals that his first wife didn’t drown. He killed her. And instead of being horrified, Not Cory is insanely happy. Because he never loved her. FUCKING WHAT?! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Then there was another reveal, which I didn’t buy at all because of all the things I’ve previously discussed in regards to Maxim. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ He tells Not Cory he loves her. It’s almost laughable. He treated her as if she were an errant child throughout the entire book. He even called her a child repeatedly, and condescended to her about it. Ugh. He did not once until that point show her more than a passing kindness and nothing in his manner ever, EVER spoke of love or any strong emotion at all.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />In closing, I don’t get the hype. I don’t think that this is remarkable or groundbreaking, and I don’t even think it should be classified as a romance. I think many of the themes are merely reused and reworked from earlier gothic novels, primarily Jane Eyre. In fact, this has such a remarkable amount of similarities to it, that I’ll be reading it next, and I plan on coming back and adding a section about my findings. <br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12951354594060188729">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_23268258">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23268258" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1183516-arlene-sanders" class="left imgcol" title="Arlene Sanders"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442488094p2/1183516.jpg" alt="Arlene Sanders" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/23268258?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 27, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1183516-arlene-sanders" class="user" title="Arlene Sanders">Arlene Sanders</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/594139.Rebecca" title="Paperback 9780380730407" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1183516-arlene-sanders?shelf=book-reviews" class="actionLinkLite">book-reviews</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer23268258">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9485012691035690124">HERE IS MY HEART...<br /><br /><br /><strong>REBECCA</strong> is my favorite book of all time -- bar none.<br /><br />The opening line is famous, but I didn’t know that the first time I read it (I was about 14). I just remember that the magic began with that first line:<br /><br /><br />Last night, I dreamt I went to Manderly again....<br /><br /><br />The girl is young, clumsy, exquisitely sensitive. Impoverished and alone after her father’s death, she was employed by a wealthy and boorish social climber, Mrs. Van Hopper, and made her living as the older woman’s companion. <br /><br />Ma</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9485012691035690124">HERE IS MY HEART...<br /><br /><br /><strong>REBECCA</strong> is my favorite book of all time -- bar none.<br /><br />The opening line is famous, but I didn’t know that the first time I read it (I was about 14). I just remember that the magic began with that first line:<br /><br /><br />Last night, I dreamt I went to Manderly again....<br /><br /><br />The girl is young, clumsy, exquisitely sensitive. Impoverished and alone after her father’s death, she was employed by a wealthy and boorish social climber, Mrs. Van Hopper, and made her living as the older woman’s companion. <br /><br />Maxim de Winter, handsome, fabulously rich, and the owner of Manderly, one of the finest estates in England, crosses paths with the women in Monte Carlo.<br /><br />As the girl falls crazy in love with de Winter, revealing herself as the most flaming romantic in all of British literature, she sees him like this:<br /><br /><br />He belonged to a walled city of the fifteenth century, a city of narrow, cobbled streets, and thin spires, where the inhabitants wore pointed shoes and worsted hose. His face was arresting, sensitive, medieval in some strange inexplicable way, and I was reminded of a portrait seen in a gallery I had forgotten where, of a certain Gentleman Unknown.<br /><br /><br />And like this:<br /><br /><br />Could one but rob him of his English tweeds, and put him in black, with lace at this throat and wrists, he would stare down at us in our new world from a long distant past—a past where men walked cloaked at night, and stood in the shadow of old doorways, a past of narrow stairways and dim dungeons, a past of whispers in the dark, of shimmering rapier blades, of silent, exquisite courtesy.<br /><br /><br />(I thought <em>I</em> was a romantic!)<br /><br />However, I never saw him the way she did. Even as a teenager, I thought de Winter was a horse’s ass and a male chauvinist pig if ever there was one, and to this day, I don't understand what women see in him:<br /><br /><br />“So Mrs. Van Hopper has had enough of Monte Carlo,” he said,<br />“and now she wants to go home. So do I. She to New York and I to Manderly. Which would you prefer? You can take your choice.”<br /><br />“Don’t make a joke about it, it’s unfair,” I said, “and I think I had better see about those tickets, and say good-bye now.”<br /><br />“If you think I’m one of the people who try to be funny at breakfast, you’re wrong,” he said. “I’m invariably ill-tempered in the early morning. I repeat to you, the choice is open to you. Either you go to America with Mrs. Van Hopper or you come home to Manderly with me.”<br /><br />"Do you mean you want a secretary or something?”<br /><br />“No, I’m asking you to marry me, you little fool.”<br /><br /><br />(Prick!)<br /><br /><br />Then this:<br /><br /><br />"So that's settled, isn't it?" he said, going on with his toast and marmalade; "instead of being companion to Mrs. Van Hopper you become mine, and your duties will be almost exactly the same. I also like new library books, and flowers in the drawing-room, and bezique after dinner. And someone to pour out my tea. . .and you must never let me run out of my particular brand of toothpaste."<br /><br /><br />(Prick!) <br /><br /><br />(Women certainly don't want male chauvinist swine as employers, but we accept them as husbands and lovers, because mostly that's all there is, so we have to make do.) <br /><br />The spirit of Rebecca herself -- the first Mrs. de Winter -- pervades the novel like a gathering storm, a painful presence for the young woman Maxim marries after Rebecca's death. Although du Maurier gave the second Mrs. de Winter an inner life of extraordinary richness and depth, the author did not give her a name. When I learned that she didn’t have a name, I gave her mine, and she became me. I think she is simply every romantic woman who ever read this remarkable novel. <br /><br />Rebecca, directed by <a title="The Thirty-nine Steps. by John Buchan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2014794.The_Thirty_nine_Steps_">Alfred Hitchcock</a>, is also my favorite film. Joan Fontaine brilliant as the second Mrs. de Winter; Laurence Olivier absolute perfection as Max. This film was released in 1940, so don't see it in a theatre filled with college students, because they will snicker in the wrong places and spoil the most poignant scenes for you.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9485012691035690124">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_186991266">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/186991266" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4632071-lora" class="left imgcol" title="Lora"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1305237530p2/4632071.jpg" alt="Lora" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/186991266?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 31, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4632071-lora" class="user" title="Lora">Lora</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1494702.Rebecca" title="Hardcover 9780385043809" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of classics from the early - mid 20th century</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lora by: </span>
      Arlene, Wendy, my love for all things Gothic and romantic
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4632071-lora?shelf=lib-read" class="actionLinkLite">lib-read</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4632071-lora?shelf=wishlist" class="actionLinkLite">wishlist</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4632071-lora?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4632071-lora?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4632071-lora?shelf=surprised-me" class="actionLinkLite">surprised-me</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4632071-lora?shelf=1001-r" class="actionLinkLite">1001-r</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer186991266">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11168243872257447108"><em>Rebecca</em> is a classic tale that weaves mystery, secrets, and romance into an intricate and stunning twine. It tells the story of a young girl who is swept off her feet by a much older man with money and possessions aplenty — and even more heartache in his recent past. <br />Since his wife's tragic death eight months ago, Maxim de Winter has been doing everything he can to forget the horrific part of his past that has left him feeling bereft of happiness and aloof from others. <br />But even with this kind of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11168243872257447108"><em>Rebecca</em> is a classic tale that weaves mystery, secrets, and romance into an intricate and stunning twine. It tells the story of a young girl who is swept off her feet by a much older man with money and possessions aplenty — and even more heartache in his recent past. <br />Since his wife's tragic death eight months ago, Maxim de Winter has been doing everything he can to forget the horrific part of his past that has left him feeling bereft of happiness and aloof from others. <br />But even with this kind of emotional baggage, the young heroine of the story — who's name is never revealed — still agrees to marry Mr. de Winter because she has already fallen in love with him. <br />When our heroine moves into Manderley, the estate where Maxim lived not so long ago with his now deceased wife, Rebecca, she soon learns the story behind her new husband's late wife's death. She learns that Rebecca died by an accidental drowning while in a boat that capsized. <br />As you can image, all of this is very disconcerting to such a young and naïve girl. And when she arrives at Manderley things are so very different from the life she had before: there's all the hustle and bustle of living in a mansion, and then there's Mrs. Danvers who doesn't like her simply because she's not Rebecca.<br />Du Maurier's <em>Rebecca</em> deals with a lot of themes and raises a lot of questions, one of the most intriguing being, <em>What happens when the woman that is haunting your husband begins haunting you, too?</em> <br /><br />For me, <em>Rebecca</em> was truly a delight. It is expertly crafted and beautifully written, and, while reading, I had one of those strange feelings you get when you think you're enjoying something too much, that you must be sinning because you simply <em>can't</em> remember the last time you enjoyed yourself so much.<br /><br />What I'm about to say isn't going to juxtapose well with my earlier comment about this being a "delight," but I shall say it anyway: This book has just a <em>little bit</em> of a depressing atmosphere to it. This is mostly because the main character is often fixated on how she'll never live up to the standards which Rebecca set before her, but it didn't bother me in the least. No, no — in fact, it only made me want to wrap the heroine in a blanket and give a her cup of hot cocoa. Some may deem her weak for not simply standing up and being everything that she can be, but I saw her as worthy of so much and strong even in her cowardice. She starts out working for a nuisance of a woman, then all of a sudden she is married to a man much older than she and with a past for which she is unsure of all the details. I really loved the heroine in this; there were several times where my heart twisted for her character and for the situation she was in.<br /><br />Do <strong>NOT</strong> read this spoiler if you've not read this book. It is the type of spoiler that will drastically take away from your enjoyment should you choose to ever read this. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[There is an exchange between our heroine and the resentful Mrs. Danvers about 2/3 of the way through the novel. During this discussion Mrs. Danvers informs our lead that while alive Rebecca had many male companions with whom she was physically intimate. Now, my first thought after reading that was, <em>How, then, could Maxim mourn her to the degree that he does? How could he be so torn about her death if she cheated on him numerous times during their marriage?</em> Well, it was because all was not what it seemed. <br />When Maxim confesses to having killed Rebecca, I LITERALLY fell out of my seat. Granted, I let myself do so. But it was so shocking I just let myself go like a slinky. It was insane. The whole time I'm thinking that Maxim will never give up Rebecca even though she is dead, that Maxim and his new wife don't have any real chance of ever being happy because, apparently, Rebecca was just too wonderful! for anyone to compare to her. <br />Ha! Ha! HAHAHA!! You can see that it has decreased my sanity a little. <br />I just couldn't believe it! That revelation meant that every paling of the face on Maxim's part, every look of worry or dread was only because <em>he</em> had killed her, not because he was sick over her death. Gah! I shall never get over the brilliancy of this <strong>Epic Twist</strong> for as long as I live. Truly. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The fact of the lead character's name never being revealed is just one of the peculiar things about this story. It is said early on that her name is often spelt incorrectly, making the reader think that it is perhaps a very unique name. My guess as to why this is is that, because the name <strong>Rebecca</strong> — and the person — is still so very dominant in the lives of the characters and in our heroine's mind, the author chose to leave out her name to add to the sense of inferiority the heroine feels towards Manderley's former mistress. Just a guess.<br /><br />FAVORITE QUOTE: <em>"I don't want you to bear this alone," I said. "I want to share it with you. I've grown up, Maxim, in twenty-four hours. I'll never be a child again."</em><br /><br />Upon closing this review I want to be very clear about something: My enthusiasm and enjoyment of this novel doesn't necessarily mean that you, the reader of this review, will feel the same about this book. <em>Rebecca</em> is very dramatic and people that don't like classics may not find as much enjoyment in it as I have. I'll freely admit that I have a penchant for things/books like this, so I'm guessing that had a lot to do with my loving this so very much. But if you're interested in this in the least, if you think this may be something you'd like, please, give it a try.<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11168243872257447108">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/111921-madeline" class="left imgcol" title="Madeline"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1220803522p2/111921.jpg" alt="Madeline" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/5964859?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 09, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/111921-madeline" class="user" title="Madeline">Madeline</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12873.Rebecca" title="Paperback 9781844080380" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=the-list" class="actionLinkLite">the-list</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=all-time-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer5964859">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16003955787424618161">"I dreamt I went to Manderley again."<br /><em>Rebecca</em> is the story of a young woman (her first name is never given) who marries wealthy Maxim de Winter, mostly to escape her life as a companion to a rich American woman. She moves with her new husband to his estate, Manderly, where she learns about her husband's previous wife, Rebecca. Although Rebecca drowned in the ocean near the house over a year ago, the house is still full of her prescence. Her old room is cleaned daily, and is left exactly the way i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16003955787424618161">"I dreamt I went to Manderley again."<br /><em>Rebecca</em> is the story of a young woman (her first name is never given) who marries wealthy Maxim de Winter, mostly to escape her life as a companion to a rich American woman. She moves with her new husband to his estate, Manderly, where she learns about her husband's previous wife, Rebecca. Although Rebecca drowned in the ocean near the house over a year ago, the house is still full of her prescence. Her old room is cleaned daily, and is left exactly the way it was when Rebecca still lived there. Her servant, the creepy and completely evil Mrs. Danvers, is still loyal to Rebecca, and the new Mrs. de Winter finds herself being compared to Rebecca by everyone she encounters. Over the course of the story, the narrator begins to inquire into Rebecca's past with her husband in an attempt to discover how she was able to captivate everyone she knew. As the story progresses, Mrs. de Winter discovers that not everyone at Manderley has been completely honest with her, and Rebecca herself is at the heart of all these secrets.<br />I really liked this book. Its plot was similar to <a title="Jane Eyre" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Jane%20Eyre">Jane Eyre</a>, but unlike Bronte, du Maurier doesn't reveal her biggest plot twist three-quarters of the way through the story - in Rebecca, the surprises keep coming until literally the last page, making it a much more enjoyable read. I also enjoyed the main character - she's clumsy and not very confident, but there's strength at her core, and this fact placed me on her side, despite her very human imperfections.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16003955787424618161">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59056501" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana" class="left imgcol" title="Tatiana"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1249783659p2/2250020.jpg" alt="Tatiana" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/59056501?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 10, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana" class="user" title="Tatiana">Tatiana</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/239113.Rebecca" title="Hardcover 9780575002326" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of classics</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tatiana by: </span>
      Hannahr, Ryan
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=mysteries-thrillers-horror" class="actionLinkLite">mysteries-thrillers-horror</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=1001" class="actionLinkLite">1001</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2010" class="actionLinkLite">2010</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer59056501">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12844293191761360992">Books like <a title="Rebecca by Daphne du Maurier" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12873.Rebecca">Rebecca</a> remind me from time to time what quality literature really is. Sometimes I forget, buried under stacks of entertaining but often poorly written popular fiction. <br /><br />At first, <a title="Rebecca by Daphne du Maurier" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12873.Rebecca">Rebecca</a> is very reminiscent of another favorite book of mine - <a title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre">Jane Eyre</a>. The main character is a young, innocent, poor girl who falls in love with a rich older man. The happiness is so near, but the shadow of the man's first wife stands in the way of it. A family secret, a haunted mansion, a deranged servant</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12844293191761360992">Books like <a title="Rebecca by Daphne du Maurier" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12873.Rebecca">Rebecca</a> remind me from time to time what quality literature really is. Sometimes I forget, buried under stacks of entertaining but often poorly written popular fiction. <br /><br />At first, <a title="Rebecca by Daphne du Maurier" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12873.Rebecca">Rebecca</a> is very reminiscent of another favorite book of mine - <a title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre">Jane Eyre</a>. The main character is a young, innocent, poor girl who falls in love with a rich older man. The happiness is so near, but the shadow of the man's first wife stands in the way of it. A family secret, a haunted mansion, a deranged servant, and a fire are also major players in the story. <br /><br />I've said it before, I personally don't mind borrowed themes, but only if done right. A talented writer can reinterpret and reinvent an old story, add new layers to it, and <a title="Daphne du Maurier" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2001717.Daphne_du_Maurier">Daphne du Maurier</a> does just that. The book is beautifully written, it is haunting, it is suspenseful.<br /><br />I also think it takes a gifted writer to make readers get attached to a character as insecure, jealous, and timid as the second Mrs. de Winter. <a title="Daphne du Maurier" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2001717.Daphne_du_Maurier">Daphne du Maurier</a> succeeds once more. The main character is very compelling and her fears are palpable. I found myself sharing the heroines insecurities (after all, why shouldn't she question her husband's feelings toward her if he treats her like a child, a pet and doesn't make an effort to let her know where he stands in regard to his first wife?), being scared of and intimidated by Mrs. Danvers, and taunted by the memories of the first (possibly superior) wife. <br /><br /><a title="Rebecca by Daphne du Maurier" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12873.Rebecca">Rebecca</a> is simply a great book all around, deservedly a masterpiece of English literature and from now on - a new favorite love story of mine, to be treasured and kept near <a title="Gone With the Wind by Margaret Mitchell" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18405.Gone_With_the_Wind">Gone With the Wind</a>, <a title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre">Jane Eyre</a>, <a title="Pride and Prejudice  by Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3084664.Pride_and_Prejudice_">Pride and Prejudice</a>, <a title="Wuthering Heights by Emily Brontë" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6185.Wuthering_Heights">Wuthering Heights</a>, and <a title="The Thorn Birds by Colleen McCullough" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3412.The_Thorn_Birds">The Thorn Birds</a>. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12844293191761360992">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_427436792">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/427436792" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13266158-samadrita" class="left imgcol" title="Samadrita"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1439470626p2/13266158.jpg" alt="Samadrita" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/427436792?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13266158-samadrita" class="user" title="Samadrita">Samadrita</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/13480820-rebecca" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=timeless-classics" class="actionLinkLite">timeless-classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=spookfest" class="actionLinkLite">spookfest</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=mystery-detective" class="actionLinkLite">mystery-detective</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=not-worth-it" class="actionLinkLite">not-worth-it</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=european-literature" class="actionLinkLite">european-literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=gothic" class="actionLinkLite">gothic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=1001-and-more" class="actionLinkLite">1001-and-more</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=britain" class="actionLinkLite">britain</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=by-women-who-matter" class="actionLinkLite">by-women-who-matter</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13266158-samadrita?shelf=500gbbw" class="actionLinkLite">500gbbw</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer427436792">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4649920888763866562">If I found myself in Interstellar's tesseract by a quirk of fate, then chances are I might leave a coded message for my younger self to read Rebecca asap. Since my ill-informed, younger self was not put off by cloyingly sentimental narrators who make the experience of <em>'tell don't show'</em> all the more grating or the gender politics underpinning a work, her reaction to the book would have been more in tune with the multitude of gushing reviews on GR and elsewhere.<br /><blockquote> <em>My heart, for all its anxiety and d</em></blockquote></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4649920888763866562">If I found myself in Interstellar's tesseract by a quirk of fate, then chances are I might leave a coded message for my younger self to read Rebecca asap. Since my ill-informed, younger self was not put off by cloyingly sentimental narrators who make the experience of <em>'tell don't show'</em> all the more grating or the gender politics underpinning a work, her reaction to the book would have been more in tune with the multitude of gushing reviews on GR and elsewhere.<br /><blockquote>
  <em>My heart, for all its anxiety and doubt, was light and free. I knew then that I was no longer afraid of Rebecca. I did not hate her any more. Now that I knew her to have been evil and vicious and rotten I did not hate her any more. She could not hurt me.</em>
</blockquote><br />Rebecca's saving grace is the authorial decision to leave the immature, young bride of Maxim de Winter nameless (let's call her Mary Lu shall we?) and deploy Manderley as a ubiquitous metaphor for the complete power that Rebecca wielded over the hearts of those who lived in her shadow. Everything else the book conjures up in the name of mystery and imagery and Gothic spookiness left me cold and distinctly unmoved - the efforts at creating a sinister environment shrouded in mystery, governed by hidden malignant forces, the detailing of Manderley's extravagantly decorated interiors, primly maintained gardens and beaches, the stilted characterization, the yawn-inducing, uneventful plot progression. <br /><br />Rebecca, the erstwhile mistress of Manderley, served as my only incentive to plod on. And yet the sole character with the kind of layered complexity that would have made the reading of this worthwhile is pigeonholed as a vamp and denied her humanity just so the second Mrs de Winter could have her existence validated in the end. Rebecca's crime? Adultery, being secure in the knowledge of her sexual prowess, possible animal cruelty, projecting a rock-solid self will, and possessing a domineering persona which brings the lilliputian stature of the rest of the two-dimensional characters in focus with embarrassing clarity. Her punishment? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[Getting murdered after contracting a terminal illness. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />As if this reluctance to develop fully realized characters wasn't enough, du Maurier added to my chagrin by accomplishing the complete sabotage of the concept of <em>'sisterhood'</em> as well. <br />All the while one wishes for Mary Lu to grow a spine and a tongue capable of intelligent speech. But it is only after her husband confesses to never loving Rebecca that she starts to develop confidence in herself. As if Rebecca's entire self worth is hinged on her husband's opinion of her and the mere possibility of Mary Lu's being the sole recipient of Maxim de Winter's 'love' and approval makes her a much worthier person than Rebecca. On top of this obnoxious insinuation that the naive protagonist's self determination is and should be dependent on how she measures up to Rebecca in terms of <em>'feminine virtue'</em>, du Maurier makes her inexplicably impervious to the gravity of a murder confession. The narrator also never doubts the legitimacy of her husband's right to escape conviction by law for a terrible crime. So basically, the Cinderella-stand-in's character growth comes at the cost of implicit character assassination of Rebecca and all that she stood for. And this is exactly the kind of subliminal sexism woven into a plot that sets my teeth on edge.<br /><br />Further, a woman's murder is normalized on the assumption that she provoked her assailant into doing it when the provocation is only of a psychological nature, not the physical kind which may vindicate aggression to fend off an attacker. This sounds way too much like <em>'she was wearing a short skirt/drunk/out at night, she was asking for it'</em> logic to me.<br /><br />So Rebecca, all your mystery-horror-suspense shenanigans and vacuous, banal philosophizing may have counted as literary merit in my eyes had I read you five years ago. I'm much too jaded to like you anymore though.<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4649920888763866562">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4094337205275666604">Manderley... a mist on the hill, an unreal mansion, dark, threatening, unfathomable, unreachable, a thing that can not exist, only seen through a dream, a mirage, that will pass into the night, and float into nothingness, a nightmarish, chilling legend, that will continue, but the reality never discovered, for a myth has no answers just questions...Maximilian (Maxim) de Winter is a sad English widower, at 42, living an aimless life, travelling to forget, but can't, staying in a Monte Carlo hotel</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4094337205275666604">Manderley... a mist on the hill, an unreal mansion, dark, threatening, unfathomable, unreachable, a thing that can not exist, only seen through a dream, a mirage, that will pass into the night, and float into nothingness, a nightmarish, chilling legend, that will continue, but the reality never discovered, for a myth has no answers just questions...Maximilian (Maxim) de Winter is a sad English widower, at 42, living an aimless life, travelling to forget, but can't, staying in a Monte Carlo hotel, just before W W 2, that has had better days. His face too revealing, showing the tragic situation he must face. A young lady half his age, is working for the implacable, unfeeling American, Mrs. Van Hopper, a middle aged woman, as a paid companion, servant and punching bag. Two souls, Max and the girl, who need comfort, happiness and yes, love, in this brutal world, they find each other and hope arrives. A quick marriage (Mrs.Van Hopper is amazed), a short honeymoon in Venice, and they're back after a few weeks, in their native England, the wild coast, of Cornwall, in the southwest of the country, far from busy, noisy, polluted, London. A quiet, hidden place, clean, too, where nothing occurs, enormous Manderley, centuries old, the home of the de Winter's family, for many generations, surrounded by thick, impenetrable woods, exotic flowers, exquisite butterflies playing in the air, beautiful green lawns, colorful birds singing sweetly, the fragrant winds blowing from the nearby sea, fishing boats traversing the horizon. Mrs.Danvers the evil looking housekeeper, the Keeper of the Flame, takes an immediate dislike of the second Mrs. de Winter, how can such a timid, uneducated, unlovely girl, take over for Rebecca? The bold, intelligent beauty, the prettiest woman in the area, Rebecca, loved by all. There are disturbing secrets kept deep in Manderley, where the bride often loses her way, not spoken about by the servants and friends of Maxim, he often disappears into himself, what is he thinking about, Rebecca? How can the new, awkward bride, compete with a phantom, without any weaknesses? Beatrice, Maxim's talkative sister, knows things but will not tell them to the second Mrs. de Winter, Jack Favell, Rebecca's first cousin comes to the mansion without her knowledge, there is something she doesn't like, about him, a drunkard, daring playboy, and incessant flirt, with a mysterious connection to Rebecca, Maxim hates him ( why?) and scolds Mrs.Danvers, his friend, for receiving him at Manderley. Walking on a pebble beach below the house, with her only friend, the constantly amusing, faithful, family dog, which she had tirelessly followed, Jasper, to the tiny harbor where Rebecca's small boat was last anchored, before being lost at sea, in an "accident". Ben a retarded man of the neighborhood, is there too, speaking gibberish but is it, or more dark secrets, divulged, she the bride sees a cottage falling into ruin, that Maxim avoids, the curious girl enters, finds many of Rebecca's things lying around, always objects that remind her, she will never be the real Mrs. de Winter, who still from the grave... dominates the premises.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_454560163">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/454560163" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl" class="left imgcol" title="Cheryl"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442870534p2/11255849.jpg" alt="Cheryl" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/454560163?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 06, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl" class="user" title="Cheryl">Cheryl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/594139.Rebecca" title="Paperback 9780380730407" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=mesmerizing" class="actionLinkLite">mesmerizing</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=on-women" class="actionLinkLite">on-women</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=classic-lit" class="actionLinkLite">classic-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=the-psyche" class="actionLinkLite">the-psyche</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=on-memory" class="actionLinkLite">on-memory</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer454560163">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8611444938165282487">Manderley, <em> <strong>"today we we pass on, we see it no more, and we are different, changed in some infinitesimal way. We can never be quite the same again." </strong> </em> It's such a sensation, when you read a book and a place is so memorable that it stays with you. To have place, plot, and person in such congruency, this is a treat; it is when you know an author is at her best. When I remember this book, I won't think of Rebecca, the deceased wife, nor will I recall the nameless narrator and gullible young bride o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8611444938165282487">Manderley, <em>
  <strong>"today we we pass on, we see it no more, and we are different, changed in some infinitesimal way. We can never be quite the same again." </strong>
</em> It's such a sensation, when you read a book and a place is so memorable that it stays with you. To have place, plot, and person in such congruency, this is a treat; it is when you know an author is at her best. When I remember this book, I won't think of Rebecca, the deceased wife, nor will I recall the nameless narrator and gullible young bride of the rich Max de Winter. Instead, I will remember Manderley and everything that happened there that is so palpable within this first person narrative. If I do think about these characters, I will think of them when I think of Manderley. And this is strange, because I think this is the first book that has placed such a reminder of place within my reading memory.<br /><br />I love my copy, because not only does it have an author's note from Daphne du Maurier, which tells of her writing experience, but there's also an essay she wrote of her own house, Menabilly, the "house of secrets" that Manderley is modeled after. She saw the house after it lay in ruins and she visited it for years, feeling somehow drawn to the forsaken mansion. Later, it would become hers. She was thirty years old and stationed with her soldier husband in Alexandria, when she began writing this story. "I lose myself in the plot as it unfolds, and only when the book is finished do I lay it aside," she said. Judging from the splay of this plot, it is obvious that she did lose herself in it. Two hundred and seventy pages in, a new story arc emerges and yet manages to remain true to the rest of the book. And Manderley is still the main character.<br /><br />Manderley, its <em>
  <strong>"colour and scent and sound, rain and the lapping of water, even the mists of autumn and the smell of the flood tide, these are memories of Manderley that will not be denied."</strong>
</em> In the midst of scent and sound, there is mystery and deceit. The glorious mansion has its hidden flaws, its dark secrets, and as the story unfolds, the narrator, a young, poor, orphan, strives to become the sophisticated wife that she thinks her older husband desires. She is alarmingly annoying, and her housekeeper, Mrs. Danvers, peculiarly frightful, yet what I found fascinating is how characters and themes seem to be balanced on a beam. <br /><br />What makes you happy? What terrifies you? How do you deal with trauma? How do you deal with guilt and shame? Do you try to fit in, or do you choose to become the most comfortable with who you are? What is the essence of partnership? What about consequences? Who are you, without everything and everyone around you, who are you, really?<br /><em>
  <blockquote>When people suffer a great shock, like death, or the loss of a limb, I believe they don't feel it just at first. If your hand is taken from you you don't know, for a few minutes, that your hand is gone. You go on feeling the fingers. You stretch and beat them on the air, one by one, and all the time there is nothing there, no hand, no fingers.</blockquote>
</em><br />Gloom resides at Manderley, and because of this, we view the mansion from a distance, in retrospective narrative tense. Manderley, <em>
  <strong>"secretive and silent as it had always been…a jewel in the hollow of a hand."</strong>
</em> Our protagonist has experienced pain and trauma and love and life and she can never go home again, this much is clear from the first sentence, <em>
  <strong>"Last night I dreamt I went to Manderley again."</strong>
</em></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8611444938165282487">...more</a>

          </span>
