<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_455093723">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/455093723" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2204947-duchess-nicole" class="left imgcol" title="Duchess Nicole"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1438371485p2/2204947.jpg" alt="Duchess Nicole" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/455093723?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 04, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2204947-duchess-nicole" class="user" title="Duchess Nicole">Duchess Nicole</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2204947-duchess-nicole?shelf=e-pcs" class="actionLinkLite">e-pcs</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2204947-duchess-nicole?shelf=dead-tree-books" class="actionLinkLite">dead-tree-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2204947-duchess-nicole?shelf=genre-mystery-suspense" class="actionLinkLite">genre-mystery-suspense</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2204947-duchess-nicole?shelf=highly-rated" class="actionLinkLite">highly-rated</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2204947-duchess-nicole?shelf=genre-classics" class="actionLinkLite">genre-classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2204947-duchess-nicole?shelf=read-in-2013" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2204947-duchess-nicole?shelf=don-t-know-why-i-loved-it" class="actionLinkLite">don-t-know-why-i-loved-it</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2204947-duchess-nicole?shelf=self-contained" class="actionLinkLite">self-contained</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer455093723">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8680837049604950456"><img class="escapedImg" src="%3Ca%20href=" /><img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380397515i/767104.jpg" /><br /><br />Dark, Gothic, mysterious...you can say all of this about Rebecca, but I have to say that none of these really does the book justice. <br /><br />Yes, it's got some <strong>dark</strong> to it. It's a story of the second wife...the young and naive bride of the rich, powerful Maximus deWinter. A tragic hero whose first wife died a bit less than a year ago. Maxim seems in turns devastated, angry, and confused about Rebecca. And in turn, our heroine...whom we never do learn the name of...what's up with that? My GR friend Cathy</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8680837049604950456"><img class="escapedImg" src="%3Ca%20href=" /><img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380397515i/767104.jpg" /><br /><br />Dark, Gothic, mysterious...you can say all of this about Rebecca, but I have to say that none of these really does the book justice. <br /><br />Yes, it's got some <strong>dark</strong> to it. It's a story of the second wife...the young and naive bride of the rich, powerful Maximus deWinter. A tragic hero whose first wife died a bit less than a year ago. Maxim seems in turns devastated, angry, and confused about Rebecca. And in turn, our heroine...whom we never do learn the name of...what's up with that? My GR friend Cathy gave me her theory, and I wholeheartedly agree, that this glaring omission was a way for this woman to remain stripped of her own identity, immersed in a world in which she is to forever remain in the shadow of the woman who came before...Rebecca.<br /><br />Rebecca<br />REBECCA<br /><strong>REBECCA</strong><br /><br />I almost got sick of the name. Her metaphorical ghost was everywhere. <br /><br /><strong>"Dear God, I did not want to think about Rebecca. I wanted to be happy, to make Maxim happy, and I wanted us to be together. There was no other wish in my heart but that. I could not help it if she came to me in thoughts, in dreams. I could not help it if I felt like a guest in Manderley, my home, walking where she had trodden, resting where she had lain. I was like a guest, biding my time, waiting for the return of the hostess..."</strong><br /><br />Granted, the heroine (whom I'll refer to as Mrs. deWinter), is very much a doormat. She is exactly the type of person to let herself be pushed into the background. From the servants constant allusions to the way things were done by "Mrs. de Winter" (i.e., <strong>REBECCA!</strong>), to Mrs. Danvers, the creepy housekeepers obvious ploys to make Mrs. deWinter uncomfortable in her new home. Even Maxim at times made me question her place in her new life. Sometimes he stuck up for her in subtle ways, but more often, he is an absent character, an absent husband, and even as Mrs. deWinter proclaims her immature, everlasting love and devotion, I couldn't help but feel uneasy about him. It seems that there is always some kind of wall between him and his new young wife. Not only is he twice her age, but he holds on to the past too much. <br /><br /><strong>"He did not belong to me at all, he belonged to Rebecca. He still thought about Rebecca. He would never love me because of Rebecca. She was in the house still, a Mrs. Danvers had said; she was in that room in the west wing, she was in the library, in the morning room, in the gallery above the hall...And in the garden, and in the woods, and down in the stone cottage on the beach. Her footsteps sounded in the corridors, her scent lingered on the stairs. The servants obeyed her orders still, the food we ate was the food she liked. Her favorite flowers filled the rooms...Rebecca was still mistress of Manderley. Rebecca was still Mrs. de Winter."</strong><br /><br /><br />The biggest thing about this novel is that <strong>
  <em>EVERYTHING IS NOT AS IT SEEMS.</em>
</strong> In fact, almost nothing is as you think it is. And while the first seventy five percent of the book is dedicated to proving how self deprecating and weak willed the heroine is, the last of the book takes all of your preconceptions and throws them into the ocean to drown with Rebecca!!!! Danvers is still creepy and evil, but I think above all, she's just crazy. Maxim isn't at all indifferent or unsympathetic, or even unloving. In fact, at times I think he is more vulnerable than the new Mrs. deWinter. <br /><br />There are secrets within the halls of Manderley, this gorgeous, sprawling house amid lush gardens and alongside the tumultuous sea. Honestly, I expected that the Gothic manor home would be dark and dreary, spooky and scary, but that was not at all the case. Manderley is actually portrayed as beautiful and refined, a well run and respected estate...almost perfection. It is regarded by the English as near perfection, just as they regarded Rebecca. So the way that you Manderley is described is this:<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="%3Ca%20href=" /><img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380397515i/767105.jpg" /><br /><br />But the FEEL of Manderley is this:<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="%3Ca%20href=" /><img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380397516i/767106.jpg" /><br /><br />Because the overall mystery and suspense behind this book lends a sinister vibe to the de Winter's marriage, turning every word from the servants, their friends, and even each other, into phrases with hidden meaning and hidden agendas. What should have been a triumphant rags to riches story for this young girl left alone in the world who catches the eye of the rich and handsome man...no, no, no...it's twisted into something dark.<br /><br />I can see how this became one of the defining books of the twentieth century. It's just a bit racier than the older classics, with more romance, more touchy-feely (she puts his hand on his knee, oh my!), more gruesome, and maybe a little less PC. Though definitely not PC by today's standards. But the wording and the prose are much easier to read than many of the classics that I've read recently, and the mystery surrounding Manderley and this odd collection of people is truly captivating. I don't tend to even like mysteries all that much, and let me assure you...above all; the romance and the suspense, this is for sure a mystery. There are revelations upon revelations. <br /><br />I was bored to tears for about three chapters. And then we travel to Manderley and the plot thickens **insert evil laugh** and before I know it, I'm gasping and making my own little predictions...some that I guess right, and others that are complete shockers. And the END, <br /><br /><strong>AAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHHH!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!</strong> Authors just don't end book like that nowadays...there would be quite the uproar, I think. But when it comes to these old books that paved the way for modern fiction...they were truly in a class all their own.<br /><br />I'm so glad I finally took the plunge!!!<br />*********************************************************<br /><br />This was listed as one of the most popular romance novels ever written, along with Pride and Prejudice. <br /><br />**Buddy read with Karen, Cathy, Tea, and Dorota**</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8680837049604950456">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1264129956">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1264129956" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/19169870-scarlet" class="left imgcol" title="Scarlet"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451105246p2/19169870.jpg" alt="Scarlet" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1264129956?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 06, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/19169870-scarlet" class="user" title="Scarlet">Scarlet</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/17899948-rebecca" title="ebook 9780316323703" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/19169870-scarlet?shelf=rc-16" class="actionLinkLite">rc-16</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1264129956">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12619167759797918226"><strong> <em>"Hemos salido vencedores de la prueba, aunque no hayamos escapado ilesos"</em> </strong><br /><br />Este es uno de esos libros que conviene iniciar sabiendo lo mínimo, aún con lo complicado que puede ser. Desde las primera páginas lo que destaca es la inestabilidad de nuestra desconocida narradora, una chica joven e insegura (increiblemente necesitada de un amiga/guía para su nueva vida) que, conforme pasan los meses desde que conoce a Maxim de Winter en Monte Carlo hasta que finaliza la historia, tiene un gran cambio.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12619167759797918226"><strong>
  <em>"Hemos salido vencedores de la prueba, aunque no hayamos escapado ilesos"</em>
</strong><br /><br />Este es uno de esos libros que conviene iniciar sabiendo lo mínimo, aún con lo complicado que puede ser. Desde las primera páginas lo que destaca es la inestabilidad de nuestra desconocida narradora, una chica joven e insegura (increiblemente necesitada de un amiga/guía para su nueva vida) que, conforme pasan los meses desde que conoce a Maxim de Winter en Monte Carlo hasta que finaliza la historia, tiene un gran cambio. Había veces que quería que le fuera bien porque parecía un venado perdido en la carretera, otras quería gritarle que no fuera estúpida y otras simplemente me dejaba pensando "¡¡que mujer!!" (no en el buen sentido) pero al final es ella y como describe <strong>sus obsesiones</strong> lo que hacía que yo quisiera seguir leyendo, era imposible no querer saber lo que sucedería.<br /><br /><strong>
  <em>"Pertenecía al pasado, ya sólo era un recuerdo"</em>
</strong><br /><br />Maxim, es el tipo de personaje que detestas pero necesitas saber de él. Es frío, pensativo, pasivo y observador. Trata a nuestra narradora como si fuera su hija más que si fuera su esposa y que la va alejándo de su mundo constantemente ¿Sus razones para hacerlo son justificadas? Sí, tras de si carga con un pasado que lo atormentará constantemente y que difícilmente lo dejara ser féliz.<br /><br /><strong>
  <em>"¡Qué estrecho era el puente que unía la realidad con lo que pudo haber sido!"</em>
</strong><br /><br />Y luego está nuestra verdadera protagonista <strong>Manderley</strong>, esta casa es la encargada de hacer girar la historia, su belleza e imponencia es quien da la pauta para los sucesos que inicialmente atormentan a la Sra. de Winter. Es de hecho gracias a Manderley que aparece el fantasma de Rebeca, la principal antagonista. Una mujera bella, agradable, envidiable, inteligente con más facetas de las que lector puede preveer inicialmente pero que, llegado un momento, se convierte en un espectro para todos, incluyendo a los espectadores.<br /><br /><strong>
  <em>"Acaso yo la obsesionaba a ella como ella a mí. ¿Me miraba desde lo alto de la galería, como había dicho la señora Danvers, y sentada junto a mí cuando me ponía a escribir mis cartas en su escritorio? Aquel impermeable que me puse, el pañuelo que usé... eran suyos. Tal vez me viera cogerlos. Jasper había sido su perro y ahora corría detrás de mí. Las rosas eran suyas y ahora las cortaba yo. ¿Me odiaba y me temía como yo a ella? ¿No hubiera querido ver a Maxim solo en la casa? Yo podía luchar contra los vivos, mas no contra los muertos.[...]Ella y yo no podíamos luchar. Era demasiado fuerte para mí"</em>
</strong><br /><br />La manera en que esta historia esta escrita es atrapante, las últimas 150 páginas son las que conforman plenamente el final y le dan nuevos matices a los personajes, de hecho creo que uno de los mejores finales que he leído a pesar de no ser inesperado. Lo que más me molesto fue la relación de la narradora con Maxim, condescendiente, obsesiva, posesiva y un tanto agresiva<br />hacen que su interacción la detestará pero que no pudiera esperar a ver esos momentos.<br /><br /><strong>
  <em>"¿Cree usted que nos estará viendo ahora?¿Cree usted que los muertos vuelven y vigilan a los vivos?"</em>
</strong><br /><br />Una historia muy recomendada si no te molestan las descripciones largas de lugares porque, como dije, aquí la verdadera reína es la casa.<br /><br /><strong>
  <em>"Rebeca, siempre Rebeca. Jamás me libraría de Rebeca"</em>
</strong><br /><br /><strong>
  <em>"Es fácil perder el juici cuando llevamos un demonio dentro"</em>
</strong><br /><br /><br /><em> [?] 2016 Reading Challenge: 08. Un libro ambientado en Europa </em><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12619167759797918226">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1255375170">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1255375170" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8406322-desislava" class="left imgcol" title="Desislava"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1416054495p2/8406322.jpg" alt="Desislava" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1255375170?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 30, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8406322-desislava" class="user" title="Desislava">Desislava</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12873.Rebecca" title="Paperback 9781844080380" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/8406322-desislava?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1255375170">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10759280725541313384"><em> <blockquote>“Happiness is not a possession to be prized, it is a quality of thought, a state of mind.”</blockquote> </em><br /><em>Rebecca</em> has been on my to read list for years (literally) and I'm glad I finally read it.<br /><br /><strong>The strengths of this book are</strong> its remarkable organization, its prose which often times caused me to stop to ponder a quote or a particular idea, the brilliantly vivid descriptions, some literary risks that the author took that worked, and most of all, its stabbing ending. <br />While it has often been described as a page t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10759280725541313384"><em>
  <blockquote>“Happiness is not a possession to be prized, it is a quality of thought, a state of mind.”</blockquote>
</em><br /><em>Rebecca</em> has been on my to read list for years (literally) and I'm glad I finally read it.<br /><br /><strong>The strengths of this book are</strong> its remarkable organization, its prose which often times caused me to stop to ponder a quote or a particular idea, the brilliantly vivid descriptions, some literary risks that the author took that worked, and most of all, its stabbing ending. <br />While it has often been described as a page turner, I can say that I was able to put it down during the first half relatively easily, but the last fourth of the book I could not put down. I liked the big plot twist at the end. To all of those who haven't read it, I'll kept it a secret. But let me tell you, you'll be in for a shock when you find the real reason of Rebecca's death. I do agree with the other reviewers who said that another chapter could have been added to the ending. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ I would like to know how Mrs de Winter and Maxim’s marriage was different after the trial and fire. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><strong>The weaknesses of the story are:</strong> <br /><blockquote>
<strong>1)</strong> The narrator of the book, who is never named (the second Mrs de Winter) compares herself to Rebecca and overtime gets depressed. This is about 60% of the book and sometimes I got bored. Also, I gave this book 4 out of 5 stars because occasionally, there were long paragraphs about the narrorator's fantasies that were just out of place and weren't of any use in the plot. <br /><br /><strong>2) </strong> Several of the characters aren't fully developed. For example, Mrs. Danvers (Rebecca's personal maid) - I wanted to know more about her.<br /><br /><strong>3)</strong> I never developed a very firm opinion of the narrator.</blockquote><br />I hope to read this again someday, knowing what I know about the ending, to see if there are any events that I interpret differently. It's a very, very good book, and I'm anxious to see the movie Hithcock made based on it. <br /><br /><em>
  <blockquote>“I wondered why it was that places are so much lovelier when one is alone.”</blockquote>
</em><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10759280725541313384">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_894780092">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/894780092" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/12222982-roya" class="left imgcol" title="Roya"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1452077290p2/12222982.jpg" alt="Roya" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/894780092?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/12222982-roya" class="user" title="Roya">Roya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12873.Rebecca" title="Paperback 9781844080380" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/12222982-roya?shelf=quite-good" class="actionLinkLite">quite-good</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer894780092">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12841112875409128982">I've been in a bit of reading slump lately. This was the third book I tried to read in the past week and I surprisingly finished it. I'd heard about it years ago and never heard a bad thing about it. Despite my recent mood, I can still tell a good book when I read one.<br /><br />First off, all of the characters, at least in my opinion, sucked. There wasn't a single character I liked. Perhaps Beatrice, but even then she has less tact than me, which is truly saying something. The worst of the characters is R</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12841112875409128982">I've been in a bit of reading slump lately. This was the third book I tried to read in the past week and I surprisingly finished it. I'd heard about it years ago and never heard a bad thing about it. Despite my recent mood, I can still tell a good book when I read one.<br /><br />First off, all of the characters, at least in my opinion, sucked. There wasn't a single character I liked. Perhaps Beatrice, but even then she has less tact than me, which is truly saying something. The worst of the characters is Rebecca herself, but they were all awful, so it's hard to say. It was very frustrating to read in that sense, but either way I just don't think it's worth five stars.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1440709643ra/16012998.gif" /><br /><br />Of course, as usual, I made it my duty to find out the biggest spoiler to prevent any iota of surprise from coming my way. (Surprise is awful in every sense. Make it go away, please.) Even then it still maintained its mystery. I didn't really know how it was going to end until the last page.<br /><br />The afterword in my edition by Sally Beauman is excellent. I found it interesting how similar the story is to du Maurier's life. Mrs de Winter's social skills (or lack thereof) belong to her, as do Rebecca's love for sailing and bisexuality (which is heavily hinted at throughout the book). Manderley was actually based off of Menabilly, which du Maurier leased and lived in for over twenty years. She used the same location for some of her other novels.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440711128i/16013109._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><em>(Daphne du Maurier at Menabilly with her children c. 1944.)</em><br /><br />While it wasn't a great book in the sense that I entirely loved it, it was great in the sense that it's a powerful book that makes you think. Definitely a classic and definitely something I'm glad I read. At first I was concerned that it was going to be one of <em>those</em> romance novels, but fortunately it wasn't in the least.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1421932343ra/13430638.gif" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12841112875409128982">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_206420163">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/206420163" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1886642-catie" class="left imgcol" title="Catie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1385169623p2/1886642.jpg" alt="Catie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/206420163?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 12, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1886642-catie" class="user" title="Catie">Catie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Catie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2467655-jo">Jo</a>
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=read-in-2011" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=mystery-thriller-noir" class="actionLinkLite">mystery-thriller-noir</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=horror" class="actionLinkLite">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer206420163">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1823760150093608479">I’ve always been fond of stories about girls lifting themselves up by their bootstraps: girls who have meager beginnings and no expectations, girls born into poverty, orphan girls, slave girls, girls who have a fire inside, a completely consuming drive to succeed.<br /><br />This is the most twisted, perverted version of that story I’ve ever read.<br /><br />Our narrator is a nameless, spineless, child-like girl who vacillates between rosy colored visions of her future, and harsh self-flagellation. She makes wild and n</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1823760150093608479">I’ve always been fond of stories about girls lifting themselves up by their bootstraps: girls who have meager beginnings and no expectations, girls born into poverty, orphan girls, slave girls, girls who have a fire inside, a completely consuming drive to succeed.<br /><br />This is the most twisted, perverted version of that story I’ve ever read.<br /><br />Our narrator is a nameless, spineless, child-like girl who vacillates between rosy colored visions of her future, and harsh self-flagellation. She makes wild and negative assumptions about everyone around her. She likes to be led by the hand, and can’t seem to make even the smallest of decisions on her own.<br /><br />My pity and disgust for this girl ran deep, but by the end of this tale I had to give her my grudging respect. God knows she earned it.<br /><br />As the story opens, she is a menial companion to an obnoxious woman and suffers the abuse and embarrassment brought about by her employer’s self-importance. By chance, they meet the grave and grieving Maxim de Winter and soon our girl is taking drives with him, eating dinner, laughing, falling hopelessly in love.<br /><br /><em>”Could one but rob him of his English tweeds, and put him in black, with lace at his throat and wrists, he would stare down at us in our new world from a long distant past – a past where men walked cloaked at night, and stood in the shadow of old doorways, a past of narrow stairways and dim dungeons, a past of whispers in the dark, of shimmering rapier blades, of silent, exquisite courtesy.”</em><br /><br />If only he weren’t old enough to be her father and situated well above her station. But then, the miraculous happens: he asks her to marry him. He sweeps her away to his palatial home and sets her up in a life of luxury.<br /><br />If this were a romance novel, it would end right there. But it’s not. <br /><br />The writing is deliciously stomach-churning. I never thought that such mundane acts as having tea, taking a walk, or answering the phone could be the stuff of nightmares, but she manages to infuse every single word of this novel with incredible tension and malice.<br /><br />Every character is complex and interesting and I felt sympathy for them all at one point or another (Yes, even you, Mrs. Danvers! Don’t scowl at me like that; it’s true.) <br /><br />The last one hundred pages or so had me gnawing on everything in sight with the sheer suspense and anxiety of it all. And the ending? Was just perfection.<br /><br /><u>Perfect Musical Pairing</u><br /><br />Kay Starr – <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vnUJp9KwbH8">Stormy Weather</a><br /><br />This was performed in 1933 as a part of the Cotton Club Parade in New York’s Cotton Club. I love that this song is from the same time period as this book. The lyrics are mournful and dark, which definitely fits the mood. But the thing that gets me the most is the thought of this song echoing out of the darkness through hidden stone passages, from the abandoned rooms of the West Wing.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1823760150093608479">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/21992464-helen-the-bookowl" class="user" title="helen the bookowl">helen the bookowl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1199422188">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4805825639707180807">This book immediately grabbed my attention because it has a very dark and sinister tone to it. It deals with Manderley, a great estate in England housing Max de Winter and his household. However, the story starts at a different place and with other characters who slowly become involved with Manderley and its mysteries. <br />This story is very Victorian and I loved it. It gradually became darker and darker, and it kept the reader guessing at what would happen next. Especially Daphne du Maurier's way o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4805825639707180807">This book immediately grabbed my attention because it has a very dark and sinister tone to it. It deals with Manderley, a great estate in England housing Max de Winter and his household. However, the story starts at a different place and with other characters who slowly become involved with Manderley and its mysteries. <br />This story is very Victorian and I loved it. It gradually became darker and darker, and it kept the reader guessing at what would happen next. Especially Daphne du Maurier's way of writing and conveying stories thrilled me and I often found myself underlining passages. <br />However, this perfectly dark story also raised a lot of serious questions in my mind when it came to the main characters' behaviour and attitude to different scenarios, and the ending was kind of vague in my opinion. Nevertheless, I very much enjoyed this book, and I will definitely return to it when I need a mysterious, creepy and dark story.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Ghost hunters, McMansion lovers, Gothic chicks</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=mystery-suspense" class="actionLinkLite">mystery-suspense</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer844462572">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13889070701523159021">I can't recall what possessed me to bump Daphne du Maurier's 1938 Gothic mystery so far up my reading list. <em>Rebecca</em> was the source material for the Academy Award winning Best Picture of 1940, directed by Alfred Hitchcock and starring Joan Fontaine &amp; Laurence Olivier, a film I recall being visually stunning but very un-Hitchcockian in its plot development. It had more in common with <em>Gone With the Wind</em> than <em>Strangers on a Train</em>. My urge to give more female authors an honest read and to find so</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13889070701523159021">I can't recall what possessed me to bump Daphne du Maurier's 1938 Gothic mystery so far up my reading list. <em>Rebecca</em> was the source material for the Academy Award winning Best Picture of 1940, directed by Alfred Hitchcock and starring Joan Fontaine &amp; Laurence Olivier, a film I recall being visually stunning but very un-Hitchcockian in its plot development. It had more in common with <em>Gone With the Wind</em> than <em>Strangers on a Train</em>. My urge to give more female authors an honest read and to find something to write glowing reviews about won out.<br /><br />Judging by this novel, I couldn't be a more ardent fan of du Maurier's.<br /><br /><em>Rebecca</em> begins with a sentence that's as airtight as it is intoxicating: "Last night I dreamt I went to Manderley again." The story is the first-person account of a naive and unsophisticated girl who, in the first of several bold moves by the author, is never revealed by her Christian name, only later as "Mrs. de Winter" or "the second Mrs. de Winter". She's an escort for an obnoxious American woman vacationing in Monte Carlo. The girl's employer attaches herself to any hotel guest she recognizes from the society pages and makes a victim of an English widower named Maximilian de Winter, heir to an estate in the West Country known as Manderley, a place, everyone agrees, of dreams.<br /><br />Though twice her age, de Winter is as drawn to the girl's innocence as she is his gentleness and experience. Not many afternoons in Monte Carlo pass between the time he permits her to call him "Maxim" and he offers to take her away from her dreadful employer by becoming his wife. The couple return to Manderley, where Mrs. de Winter is overwhelmed by the house with its unoccupied rooms, its staff willing to cater to her every need and neighbors requesting a social call. She immediately feels the scorn of housekeeper Mrs. Danvers, a cold hearted bitch she is certain intends to destroy her.<br /><br /><em>Rebecca</em> is a ghost story in which no visitations from beyond the grave occur. The second Mrs. de Winter is haunted by her predecessor, a combination of beauty, brains and breeding who drowned in a boating accident but lives in every furniture arrangement and in the eyes of everyone she meets at Manderley. Mrs. Danvers does rank as one of the great antagonists of literature, her battle of wills with the second Mrs. de Winter as one-sided as a cat playing with a mouse, but it is Rebecca who proves to be the force threatening the couple. I was surprised to find myself rooting for the protagonists to vanquish Rebecca and live happily ever after. Shocking how great writing can reduce me to a 16-year-old girl.<br /><br />Du Maurier has a terrific ear for dialogue and propels the story forward using a lot of it, which I always think is great. Her prose is as intoxicating as it is concise, detailing the landscapes and rooms her characters move through with lush confidence and without turning the book into a furniture catalog. Manderley is based on a Cornwall estate known as Menabilly that du Maurier became obsessed with, but instead of writing a history of the estate or the relics who lived there, she focuses on a new arrival, a rube who must pass muster and conquer her fear of the house or risk being driven out and destroyed by it. I liked that.<br /><br />I also liked how du Maurier doesn't force a conventional whodunit on the reader. Mrs. de Winter doesn't assert herself into the story as much as a contemporary heroine might. The plot materializes around her in a very effecting way. Her motivation is to make her marriage work and keep her husband happy. If she fails, she risks going back into the world where she has fewer opportunities to survive. In addition to examining what more marriages were like before women's lib, this aspect to the story elevated the tension supremely well. I'll be elevating more of du Maurier's fiction to the top of my reading list.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13889070701523159021">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_92938146">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/92938146" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3399699-rose" class="left imgcol" title="Rose"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1386022874p2/3399699.jpg" alt="Rose" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/92938146?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 20, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3399699-rose" class="user" title="Rose">Rose</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/17899948-rebecca" title="ebook 9780316323703" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=suspense" class="actionLinkLite">suspense</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=historical" class="actionLinkLite">historical</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=crime" class="actionLinkLite">crime</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=thriller" class="actionLinkLite">thriller</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=psychological" class="actionLinkLite">psychological</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=drama" class="actionLinkLite">drama</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=realistic-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">realistic-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=arc-or-galley" class="actionLinkLite">arc-or-galley</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=netgalley" class="actionLinkLite">netgalley</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=gothic" class="actionLinkLite">gothic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=all-time-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3399699-rose?shelf=books-read-then-immediately-bought" class="actionLinkLite">books-read-then-immediately-bought</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer92938146">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer97232301849386785"><strong>Initial reaction:</strong> I loved this book even years after reading it for the first time. I think Daphne du Maurier had such a strong construction with respect to the characters, sense of place, foreboding atmosphere, and symbolic texture of this entire narrative. Beautifully written.<br /><br /><strong>Full review:</strong><br /><br /><em>"They make fools of us together<br />But we always think of them<br />All their laughing and their talking and their wasting of our time<br />And it always hurts to see them now<br />That everything is different<br />We don't like to see t</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText97232301849386785"><strong>Initial reaction:</strong> I loved this book even years after reading it for the first time. I think Daphne du Maurier had such a strong construction with respect to the characters, sense of place, foreboding atmosphere, and symbolic texture of this entire narrative. Beautifully written.<br /><br /><strong>Full review:</strong><br /><br /><em>"They make fools of us together<br />But we always think of them<br />All their laughing and their talking and their wasting of our time<br />And it always hurts to see them now<br />That everything is different<br />We don't like to see their eyes cause they will never look the same."</em><br /><br />-Lyrics from "Horror Show" by The Birthday Massacre<br /><br />Citing part of these lyrics because they were running through my head thematically as I penned this review. Seems appropriate considering this is a gothic novel. =)<br /><br />Writing this review on one of my all-time favorite novels is a bit intimidating because there aren't enough proper words for me to convey how much I loved Daphne du Maurier's "Rebecca." This is a deliciously dark gothic novel with wonderfully flawed, developed characters in scenarios that are equal parts harrowing, intriguing, and frustrating. I always say that the books that I end up loving are ones that leave me thinking long after I've turned the final page, as well as those that blend quite a mixture of different elements within them. This is, in parts, a mystery, suspense, and darker romance, with qualities that are atmospheric, foreboding, and contemplative.<br /><br />I first read "Rebecca" at the age of 14 - as part of a mandatory book review assignment for my 9th grade Advanced English class. I didn't really have a large selection of books to choose from, and "Rebecca" seemed one of the most intriguing to me from the blurb. I picked it up from the school library the very next day after the assignment was given. The only available copy of the book was worn thin, the pages almost falling out from where it had been read so much. It was the old Avon edition that had this cover:<br /><br /><img width="293" class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1392949558i/8638085.jpg" /><br /><br />Writing the book report was another matter, but I loved the experience of reading "Rebecca" for the first time - for its characters, for even the suspense it kept me holding onto. Just when you think things might get easier for Maxim and the new Mrs. De Winter, it more frequently fell apart than not.<br /><br />I've read this novel multiple times since, but I think this is the first time I've fully read the work in about ten years. I'm going to approach this from my own interpretations and perspective, but there's a very nice afterword in the Little Brown and Company edition that explains the background in which du Maurier wrote "Rebecca," the thematic and symbolism of the novel, among a few other interesting tidbits.<br /><br />Let's start with the characters. What an exceptional, yet flawed cast of complex characters. The reader finds themselves following the heroine of this novel from one point to the next, and part of the intrigue lies in her identity, or lack thereof. Some call her "nameless", some call her "me", some call her the "second Mrs. De Winter" or simply "Mrs. De Winter." I'm going to refer to her as "Mrs. De Winter" for this review. She's a deeply flawed character. Sometimes you may want to throw the book at her because she tends to be submissive more often than not, not so much asserting herself where she needs to. And you could argue that "Mrs. De Winter never really comes to have her own identity during the course of events in "Rebecca". Rather, there are points where it seems like she comes into her own, grows from the experiences she endures, but yet regresses in places as the wife of Maxim De Winter and learning all the secrets that Manderley has to hold.<br /><br />She starts the novel as a naïve assistant to a rather brazen, self-centered woman. This changes when the ladies meet Maxim De Winter. Maxim is, in himself, a complicated, troubled gentleman with a brooding, sometimes temperamental presence. The heroine isn't exactly sure why, especially when the mention of Manderley seems to take him from being a charming person to one who's notably haunted. Still, the two end up enjoying each others company. By scheme of events, Mrs. De Winter ends up impulsively marrying Maxim (even if he's in his 40s and could well be a father figure to her. He reminds her as much when talking about her "innocence.").<br /><br />When the two end up returning to Manderley after their honeymoon, Mrs. De Winter has all the joy of a blushing bride approaching her new life in something she can claim as her own along with her husband. But the life she imagines and gushes over is not the one waiting for her at Manderley. It'd be one thing if it were just the routine and formalities that await her, but the people and environment of Manderley is weighed down by the memory of one single unforgettable woman.<br /><br /><em>Rebecca.</em><br /><br />Everything from the slanted curves of her writing to her noted tragic demise - Rebecca's presence is felt in every corner, even among the staff so loyal to her (especially one very obsessed Mrs. Danvers, who isn't happy with Maxim's replacement wife.) Mrs. De Winter realizes she has shoes to fill. It is frustrating in turns to see how the heroine submits to keeping everything the same and withdrawal from asserting herself, but I was fascinated by the intricate power plays that this novel showcased with Mrs. De Winter, internal and external for her part. She struggles to assert herself out of Rebecca's shadow, but more often than not - she's plagued with her own personal demons of gossip as well as chatter and expectation from people who consider her less than Rebecca. And more often than not, she earns ire for what she doesn't know about Rebecca and Maxim's respective past. Each time she pushes, though, it seems to drive Maxim further away.<br /><br />Mrs. De Winter finds comrades in Beatrice (Bee, who is Maxim's honest sister - I loved her) and Frank, Maxim's closest friend - who seems to know more than he lets on at times.<br /><br />There are some striking twists in this novel that are so well plotted and jarring that even upon this re-read, it still strikes me with the same eerieness and power it had in the first time I read the novel. I won't say more to spoil the experience, but the web weaves much deeper than anything Mrs. De Winter imagined, and while it pulls her closer to Maxim once she learns the truth of things, it also threatens to unravel her as well.<br /><br />An all time favorite of mine, notably a classic for good reason, and highly recommended.<br /><br />Overall score: 5/5 stars<br /><br />Note: I received this as an ARC from NetGalley, from the publisher Little Brown and Company.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="97232301849386785">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_351088481">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/351088481" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2411388-hannah" class="left imgcol" title="Hannah"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1272561978p2/2411388.jpg" alt="Hannah" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/351088481?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 29, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2411388-hannah" class="user" title="Hannah">Hannah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1308691.Rebecca" title="Mass Market Paperback " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=houses-as-characters" class="actionLinkLite">houses-as-characters</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=re-read" class="actionLinkLite">re-read</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=gothics" class="actionLinkLite">gothics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=2012-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2012-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer351088481">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14464212839710062517">After 5+ re-reads since my distant teen years, this book still has the power to mesmerize me.<br /><br />Definitely one of the best character studies I've ever read, and after having recently read a biography of du Maurier, I am convinced that she poured much of her own personality and insecurities into the unnamed second Mrs. de Winter. There's no denying the poignant truth of her observations on inadequacy and powerlessness. It's so realistic it hurts to read it at times throughout the story.<br /><br />Another trium</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14464212839710062517">After 5+ re-reads since my distant teen years, this book still has the power to mesmerize me.<br /><br />Definitely one of the best character studies I've ever read, and after having recently read a biography of du Maurier, I am convinced that she poured much of her own personality and insecurities into the unnamed second Mrs. de Winter. There's no denying the poignant truth of her observations on inadequacy and powerlessness. It's so realistic it hurts to read it at times throughout the story.<br /><br />Another triumphant feature of this novel is du Maurier's descriptive prose. No other author, with the exception of <a title="Mary Stewart" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15590.Mary_Stewart">Mary Stewart</a>, is able to evoke a sense of place like Daphne. I may never make it across the pond to Cornwall, but I think I have a feel for it through du Maurier's novels.<br /><br />This is a masterpiece of the genre, and one of my favorite books. I miss the genius of writers like du Maurier, especially in today's literary circus of dreck....<em>50 Shades</em>, anyone? Seriously?<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14464212839710062517">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wckElz4+jOM7m2dJa1Wns/9LrldIm4yisJZgqIxRrOCkHTtEzOyYGbdDE/IVNEEBtrxtTgFGywjan2U6QMRapQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tJhXV85qdo91bE6sMmbCuRmK9Z1AE/K2+/f8wxE+OR3RTGiEPLhidfm0OhdMByQLUH02hAnOtRyR/vlR3avPWA==')})
  }

A classic gothic romance, the story features an 18-year-old Miranda Wells who falls under the spell of a mysterious old mansion and its equally fascinating master. Tired of churning butter, weeding the garden patch, and receiving the dull young farmers who seek her hand in marriage, Miranda is excited by an invitation from the upstate New York estate of her distant relative, the intriguing Nicholas Van Ryn. Her passion is kindled by the icy fire of Nicholas, the last of the Van Ryns, and the luxury of Dragonwyck, and a way of life of which she has only dreamed. Dressed in satin and lace, she becomes part of Dragonwyck, with its Gothic towers, flowering gardens, acres of tenant farms, and dark, terrible secrets. This compelling novel paints a marvelous portrait of a country torn between freedom and feudal traditions; a country divided between the very wealthy and the very poor. Poor tenant farmers at Dragonwyck, the European royalty who visit, and American icons such as Edgar Allan Poe, Herman Melville, and the Astors are vividly brought to life. This is a heart-stopping story of a remarkable woman, her breathtaking passions, and the mystery and terror that await her in the magnificent hallways of Dragonwyck.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a Blue-eyed and high-spirited, Elfride has little experience of the world beyond, and becomes entangled with two men: the boyish architect, Stephen Smith, and the older literary man, Henry Knight. The former friends become rivals, and Elfride faces an agonizing choice. Written at a crucial time in Hardy\'s life, A Pair of Blue Eyes expresses more directly than any of his novels the events and social forces that made him the writer he was. Elfride\'s dilemma mirrors the difficult decision Hardy himself had to make with this novel: to pursue the profession of architecture, where he was established, or literature, where he had yet to make his name. It is here in this world of her own that she finds a passion that comes only with the freedom to choose, and it is this, her greatest gift, that she passes on to the only one who can understand its     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2001717.Daphne_du_Maurier">About Daphne du Maurier</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/2001717.Daphne_du_Maurier"><img alt="2001717" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1422444467p2/2001717.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer18280031115491892619">If Daphne du Maurier had written only Rebecca, she would still be one of the great shapers of popular culture and the modern imagination. Few writers have created more magical and mysterious places than Jamaica Inn and Manderley, buildings invested with a rich character that gives them a memorable life of their own.<br /><br />In many ways the life of Daphne du Maurier resembles that of a fairy tale. Born int</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18280031115491892619">If Daphne du Maurier had written only Rebecca, she would still be one of the great shapers of popular culture and the modern imagination. Few writers have created more magical and mysterious places than Jamaica Inn and Manderley, buildings invested with a rich character that gives them a memorable life of their own.<br /><br />In many ways the life of Daphne du Maurier resembles that of a fairy tale. Born into a family with a rich artistic and historical background, the daughter of a famous actor-manager, she was indulged as a child and grew up enjoying enormous freedom from financial and parental restraint. She spent her youth sailing boats, travelling on the Continent with friends, and writing stories. A prestigious publishing house accepted her first novel when she was in her early twenties, and its publication brought her not only fame but the attentions of a handsome soldier, Major (later Lieutenant-General Sir) Frederick Browning, who married her.<br /><br />Her subsequent novels became bestsellers, earning her enormous wealth and fame. While Alfred Hitchcock's film based upon her novel proceeded to make her one of the best-known authors in the world, she enjoyed the life of a fairy princess in a mansion in Cornwall called Menabilly, which served as the model for Manderley in Rebecca.<br /><br />Daphne du Maurier was obsessed with the past. She intensively researched the lives of Francis and Anthony Bacon, the history of Cornwall, the Regency period, and nineteenth-century France and England, Above all, however, she was obsessed with her own family history, which she chronicled in Gerald: a Portrait, a biography of her father; The du Mauriers, a study of her family which focused on her grandfather, George du Maurier, the novelist and illustrator for Punch; The Glassblowers, a novel based upon the lives of her du Maurier ancestors; and Growing Pains, an autobiography that ignores nearly 50 years of her life in favour of the joyful and more romantic period of her youth. Daphne du Maurier can best be understood in terms of her remarkable and paradoxical family, the ghosts which haunted her life and fiction.<br /><br />While contemporary writers were dealing critically with such subjects as the war, alienation, religion, poverty, Marxism, psychology and art, and experimenting with new techniques such as the stream of consciousness, du Maurier produced 'old-fashioned' novels with straightforward narratives that appealed to a popular audience's love or fantasy, adventure, sexuality and mystery. At an early age, she recognised that her readership was comprised principally of women, and she cultivated their loyal following through several decades by embodying their desires and dreams in her novels and short stories.<br /><br />In some of her novels, however, she went beyond the technique of the formulaic romance to achieve a powerful psychological realism reflecting her intense feelings about her father, and to a lesser degree, her mother. This vision, which underlies Julius, Rebecca and The Parasites, is that of an author overwhelmed by the memory of her father's commanding presence. In Julius and The Parasites, for example, she introduces the image or a domineering but deadly father and the daring subject of incest.<br /><br />In Rebecca, on the other hand, du Maurier fuses psychological realism with a sophisticated version of the Cinderella story. The nameless heroine has been saved from a life of drudgery by marrying a handsome, wealthy aristocrat, but unlike the Prince in Cinderella, Maxim de Winter is old enough to be the narrator's father. The narrator thus must do battle with The Other Woman - the dead Rebecca and her witch-like surrogate, Mrs Danvers - to win the love of her husband and father-figure.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.dumaurier.org/obituary.html" target="_blank">http://www.dumaurier.org/obituary.html</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18280031115491892619">...more</a>

    </div>
